Doe v. State


Docket No. 26248 , 2000 Opinion No. 92


SUPREME COURT OF IDAHO


134 Idaho 760; 9 P.3d 1226; 2000 Ida. LEXIS 95



September 6, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel Corrected September 7, 2000. Released for Publication September 29, 2000.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, State of Idaho, Ada County. Hon. John F. Dutcher, Magistrate Judge.



DISPOSITION: Appeal from order denying a motion to intervene, Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the decision of the District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, Ada County, State of Idaho, which denied appellant's motion to intervene in proceedings involving her granddaughter under the Child Protective Act, Idaho Code § 16-1601 set seq.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Department of Health and Welfare had legal custody of the child under the provisions of the Child Protective Act, Idaho Code § 16-1601 et seq. Appellant grandmother, who was the primary caregiver for the child, filed a motion to intervene in the proceedings pursuant to Idaho R. Civ. P. 24(b). Respondent father filed a motion to dismiss appellant's motion. Respondent department filed a report recommending that they seek reunification of the child with respondent father. The magistrate court denied appellant's motion to intervene. Appellant sought review. Judgment was affirmed because the Child Protective Act did not provide a conditional statutory right to intervene, and the magistrate court did not abuse its discretion in denying appellant's motion for permissive intervention.


OUTCOME: Denial of appellant's motion to intervene was affirmed because the Child Protective Act did not provide a conditional statutory right to intervene, and the magistrate court did not abuse its discretion in denying appellant's motion for permissive intervention.

 

200 of 368 DOCUMENTS


Pizzuto v. State


Docket No. 24802


SUPREME COURT OF IDAHO


134 Idaho 793; 10 P.3d 742; 2000 Ida. LEXIS 97



September 6, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied October 16, 2000. Released for Publication October 16, 2000.
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed Pizzuto v. Arave, 280 F.3d 949, 2002 U.S. App. LEXIS 1787 (9th Cir. Idaho, 2002)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of the Second Judicial District of the State of Idaho, Idaho County. Hon. George R. Reinhardt, III, District Judge.
State v. Pizzuto, 119 Idaho 742, 810 P.2d 680, 1991 Ida. LEXIS 5 (Idaho, 1991)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the district court's summary dismissal of appellant's third amended petition for post-conviction relief, Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the order of the District Court of the Second Judicial District of the State of Idaho, Idaho County, which summarily dismissed his third amended petition for post-conviction relief pursuant to Idaho Code § 19-2719.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of two counts of first-degree murder and sentenced to death on May 23, 1986. Appellant previously filed two motions for post-conviction relief, which were denied. Appellant filed an amended third petition for post-conviction relief, alleging that the prosecution had wrongfully withheld evidence of an impeaching nature which could have been used to attack the reliability of the testimony given by co-defendants and that the judge should have disqualified himself. The motion was summarily dismissed for lack of jurisdiction because appellant failed to show that he could not have raised the issues in his earlier petitions for post-conviction relief. Appellant sought review. The order was affirmed because appellant either knew or should have known about the evidence at the time of his first appeal and his failure to raise the issue previously, waived the issue for further appeal 10 years later, and appellant failed to show that the judge abused his discretion in refusing to disqualify himself.


OUTCOME: Denial of third petition for post-conviction relief was affirmed because appellant either knew or should have known about the evidence at the time of his first appeal and his failure to raise the issue previously, waived the issue for further appeal 10 years later, and appellant failed to show that the judge abused his discretion in refusing to disqualify himself.
